		<item>
		<title>Implement a backup strategy for Amazon Quick Sight BI assets</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/implement-a-backup-strategy-for-amazon-quick-sight-bi-assets/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Enrique Salgado Hernández]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 29 Jun 2026 18:15:14 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Quick Sight]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Best Practices]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">b8cb09c8c030ed3f59ef78ee5fefe89a545d01c0</guid>

					<description>In this post, we cover best practices for implementing an effective backup strategy for BI assets in Quick Sight. We start by covering the options for selecting the assets to include in your backup, then explain the high-level APIs available for that purpose, and finalize with sample code to help you get started quickly.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;&lt;span class="c-mrkdwn__draggable-link"&gt;&lt;a class="c-link" href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/quicksight/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" data-stringify-link="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/quicksight/" data-sk="tooltip_parent"&gt;Amazon Quick Sight&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&amp;nbsp;is a core feature within&amp;nbsp;&lt;span class="c-mrkdwn__draggable-link"&gt;&lt;a class="c-link" href="https://aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" data-stringify-link="https://aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/" data-sk="tooltip_parent"&gt;Amazon Quick&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&amp;nbsp;— an agentic, AI-powered digital workspace designed to maximize end-user productivity— that provides AI-powered BI capabilities through natural language queries, interactive dashboards, and embedded analytics from trusted enterprise data sources.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Quick Sight assets such as dashboards, analyses, datasets, and data sources can be backed up using the &lt;code&gt;AssetsAsBundle&lt;/code&gt; APIs described in this post. A backup strategy helps protect against accidental deletions, unintended modifications, and regional disruptions. For teams that rely on Quick Sight to support critical business decisions, a well-designed backup plan is recommended.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This post is the first in a two-part series covering backup and restore for Amazon Quick Sight BI assets:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Part 1 (this post):&lt;/strong&gt; Covers how to design and implement a backup strategy, including asset selection, the APIs available for export, and a ready-to-use sample automation tool.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Part 2:&lt;/strong&gt; Covers the restore process. You can use the backups created in Part 1 to recover assets after accidental deletion, unintended changes, or as part of a broader disaster recovery plan.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;An effective backup strategy is especially critical for organizations in heavily regulated industries such as financial services, healthcare, and energy, for multiple reasons:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Data loss prevention&lt;/strong&gt; protects against human errors, accidental deletions, and events like ransomware.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Meeting recovery objectives&lt;/strong&gt; helps organizations achieve their Recovery Point Objectives (RPO) and Recovery Time Objectives (RTO), minimizing data loss during incidents.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Audit and reporting&lt;/strong&gt; supports tracking and reporting on assets throughout their lifecycle (creation, updates, and deletion).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Increased workload resiliency&lt;/strong&gt; enables quick restoration of systems to previous states, reducing downtime and improving reliability. This aligns with the Reliability pillar of the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/wellarchitected/latest/reliability-pillar/welcome.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Well-Architected Framework&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Disaster recovery (DR) preparedness&lt;/strong&gt; provides a foundation for implementing a &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/what-is/disaster-recovery/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DR process&lt;/a&gt; that anticipates technology-related disasters and contributes to your organization’s &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/whitepapers/latest/disaster-recovery-workloads-on-aws/business-continuity-plan-bcp.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;business continuity plan (BCP)&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more information about the disaster recovery capabilities of Quick, and how to assess them against organizational requirements, see the &lt;a href="https://builder.aws.com/content/3ARv3heLpgnufWleBwpxLYmHWeu/amazon-quick-disaster-recovery-and-resiliency-guide" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick disaster recovery and resiliency guide&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we cover best practices for implementing an effective backup strategy for BI assets in Quick Sight. We start by covering the options for selecting the assets to include in your backup, then explain the high-level APIs available for that purpose, and finalize with sample code to help you get started quickly.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Backup practices for business intelligence&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;BI systems present unique business continuity challenges because of their role in supporting decision-making processes and key stakeholders. You must protect them against service disruptions by implementing an effective backup plan. Before building this plan, it’s important to understand the architecture and the dimensions to consider as part of your DR plan.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img class="aligncenter" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/05/ML-20472-1.png" alt="Diagram showing Quick Sight regional architecture with the identity Region hosting users and groups and separate analysis Regions hosting data sources, datasets, analyses, and dashboards across multiple Availability Zones" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The preceding diagram shows that Quick Sight relies on AWS’s global infrastructure across multiple AWS Regions to &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/disaster-recovery-resiliency.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;provide high availability&lt;/a&gt; for Quick Sight assets, including data sources, datasets, analyses, and dashboards.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Super-fast, Parallel, In-memory Calculation Engine (SPICE) stores and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/data-encryption.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;encrypts&lt;/a&gt; imported data with high availability (HA) through redundant copies across multiple Availability Zones (AZs) within the Quick Sight Region.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With this regional design, you can maintain resources in multiple Regions and use a secondary Region in the unlikely event of a regional outage affecting your primary BI resources.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For &lt;em&gt;user and identity management&lt;/em&gt;, Quick Sight uses a single Region that you define during the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/signing-up.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;initial account subscription process&lt;/a&gt;. The diagram shows that this Region hosts user and group identity information and must be available for users to access Quick Sight.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For example, if a user wants to access a dashboard in the eu-west-1 Region but the Quick Sight main Region is us-east-1, both Regions must be available to finish the user access flow. Quick Sight uses regional architecture with AZs for redundancy. However, if your business needs protection against the unlikely event of a regional outage, you must design your disaster recovery (DR) strategy accordingly.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tip:&lt;/strong&gt; If you’re unsure of your Quick Sight main Region, you can retrieve this information by running the following command:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws quicksight describe-account-settings --aws-account-id XXXXXXXXXXXX --region us-east-1&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; This &lt;code&gt;aws quicksight describe-account-settings&lt;/code&gt; command specifies us-east-1 as the endpoint Region. If you receive a &lt;code&gt;200&lt;/code&gt; status, your identity Region is us-east-1. Otherwise, you receive an error like the following, which instructs you to point to your current identity Region (for example, eu-west-1):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;An error occurred (AccessDeniedException) when calling the DescribeAccountSettings operation: Operation is being called from endpoint us-east-1, but your identity region is us-east-1. Please use the eu-west-1 endpoint.&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Defining Quick Sight assets to include in the backup plan&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With a clearer understanding of Quick Sight architecture, the next step is selecting the assets to include in your backup plan, for this you can follow two strategies:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Back up specific assets:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This option is suitable when you define a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/prescriptive-guidance/latest/security-best-practices/strategy.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;backup or DR strategy&lt;/a&gt; focused on protecting critical assets for your business operations that you can conveniently restore after a disaster or accidental deletion. This includes specific dashboards (and their dependent assets) that key stakeholders use to make business decisions or that operating teams (finance, logistics, procurement, and so on) use to support continued business operation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This option is recommended when you require a straightforward backup plan and when the BI assets that are key to business continuity are a subset of all the assets available in your Quick Sight instance.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Back up all assets:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This strategy is recommended when you want to define a backup strategy that covers both versioning and potential disaster recovery. By backing up all assets, you can perform in-place rollback of any asset to a previous state if a human error causes an unintended modification or deletion. Additionally, because you have a backup of all assets in your account, you can select specific assets to restore as part of your DR plan.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This approach gives you maximum coverage but also requires more complex orchestration and automation. This post focuses on this strategy and provides sample code that you can adapt to minimize time to production.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After you select your strategy, choose the type of BI assets to export. Quick Sight offers the following asset types:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/creating-a-dashboard.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Dashboards&lt;/a&gt;: Read-only assets targeted at reader users, published from an analysis. You can also save a dashboard to an analysis to make edits.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/working-with-visuals.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Analyses and dashboards&lt;/a&gt;: An analysis is an editable version of a dashboard. Only the authors you choose can access it.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/working-with-data-sources.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Data sources&lt;/a&gt;: A data source implements the connection to your data, which can come from analytic sources such as databases or data warehouses, AWS services such as Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3), or third-party software as a service (SaaS) data providers such as Jira and ServiceNow.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/working-with-datasets.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Datasets&lt;/a&gt;: An asset type that uses a data source to access external data that you can use to prepare and structure the data that powers your analyses and dashboards.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/working-with-aws-vpc.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;VPC connections&lt;/a&gt;: A feature that you can use to integrate with your VPC resources such as databases and data warehouses that are located in that VPC or reachable from it (peered VPCs or networks connected through VPN or AWS Direct Connect).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/themes-in-quicksight.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Themes&lt;/a&gt;: A collection of styling and appearance settings that you can apply to multiple analyses and dashboards to match an aesthetic standard that meets your product or corporate branding needs.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;All these assets have dependencies between each other, with analyses and dashboards at the top of this dependency chain, as the following diagram illustrates.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img class="aligncenter" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/05/ML-20472-2.png" alt="Diagram of Quick Sight asset dependencies showing analyses and dashboards at the top, datasets in the middle, and data sources, VPC connections, and themes at the bottom" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When you choose the asset types to back up, be aware of these dependencies so you can fully restore assets from the backup. For example, when you back up a dashboard, you also need to back up its dependencies, which might include datasets, data sources, VPC connections, and a theme. The next sections explain how Quick Sight export APIs handle these dependencies.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Backup process overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The mechanism we cover in this post uses the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/developerguide/asset-bundle-ops.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AssetsAsBundle APIs&lt;/a&gt; available in Quick Sight. AssetsAsBundle APIs (also referenced as AAB APIs) are a set of high-level APIs designed to support programmatic export and import of Quick Sight resources. They cover a range of use cases such as release management, backup and restore, cross-account migration, and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/solutions/guidance/multi-account-environments-on-amazon-quicksight/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) workflows&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This set of APIs includes the following operations:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_StartAssetBundleExportJob.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;StartAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/a&gt;: Creates a package (bundle) that contains the assets exported as part of the operation. The package is a zip file with text files. The format can be either JSON or &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cloudformation/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS CloudFormation&lt;/a&gt; depending on the value specified in the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_StartAssetBundleExportJob.html#API_StartAssetBundleExportJob_RequestSyntax" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ExportFormat&lt;/a&gt; parameter. Depending on the format, you can import these assets using the AAB APIs directly or use CloudFormation infrastructure as code (IaC) for provisioning. After the asynchronous operation finishes, the system uploads the bundle to a temporary S3 location for downloading.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_StartAssetBundleImportJob.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;StartAssetBundleImportJob&lt;/a&gt;: Takes a previously exported bundle and restores the assets packed in it. You can use the import operation to define &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_AssetBundleImportJobDataSourceOverrideParameters.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;overrides for a wide&lt;/a&gt; set of parameters such as asset names and data source connection parameters (host, port, workgroup, and more).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeAssetBundleImportJob.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeAssetBundleImportJob&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeAssetBundleExportJob.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/a&gt;: Both AssetBundle operations are asynchronous. You can use these APIs to describe the operation, poll for its status, and act after it finishes. When you perform an export job, you can use &lt;code&gt;DescribeAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/code&gt; to retrieve the &lt;code&gt;DownloadUrl&lt;/code&gt; for the bundle, which is valid for 5 minutes. You can renew the URL with further calls to &lt;code&gt;DescribeAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="supported-assets-and-current-limitations-of-assetsasbundle-apis"&gt;Supported assets and current limitations of AssetsAsBundle APIs&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AssetsAsBundle APIs support a list of Quick Sight assets including analyses, dashboards, data sources, datasets, shared folders, restricted folders, refresh schedules, themes, and VPC connections. However, some asset types have limitations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Unsupported data sources:&lt;/strong&gt; Adobe Analytics, File, GitHub, Jira, Salesforce, ServiceNow, Amazon S3 (with locally uploaded &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/user/supported-manifest-file-format.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;manifest files&lt;/a&gt;), and Twitter.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Unsupported datasets:&lt;/strong&gt; Datasets that contain machine learning (ML) columns generated using inference through &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/user/sagemaker-integration.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;connected SageMaker ML models&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You must exclude these assets from your backup plan to avoid an &lt;code&gt;InvalidParameterValueException&lt;/code&gt; error when you issue the &lt;code&gt;StartAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/code&gt; operation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To work around this, you can replace unsupported data sources and datasets by following these procedures.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;For Amazon S3 data sources with local manifest files:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a new Amazon S3 data source.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Upload the manifest file to Amazon S3.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Reference the manifest file from your data source.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Replace the data source in the dependent datasets using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_UpdateDataSet.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;UpdateDataSet&lt;/a&gt; API.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;For other unsupported data sources and datasets:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Follow this procedure to transform your incompatible dataset into a compatible one:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create an analysis connected to the data source you want to support in your backup.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/tabular.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;table visual&lt;/a&gt; that displays all dataset columns.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Export the data as a CSV file.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create an Amazon S3 dataset using a manifest uploaded to Amazon S3.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Update your analyses and dashboards with the new dataset using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/replacing-data-sets.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;replace dataset functionality&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="other-assets-to-consider-as-part-of-your-backup"&gt;Other assets to consider as part of your backup&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Although Quick Sight resources are the key assets to back up, you need to include some additional resources and configurations in your backup plan for potential restore or disaster recovery situations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can export Quick Sight assets along with their permissions, including the users and groups that have access to them. You control this by setting the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_StartAssetBundleExportJob.html#API_StartAssetBundleExportJob_RequestSyntax" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;IncludePermissions&lt;/a&gt; flag to &lt;code&gt;true&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Because each Quick Sight asset is owned by a user, you need to back up users and groups to have a full and restorable backup.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AssetsAsBundle APIs don’t cover users and groups, but you can use &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeUser.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeUser&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeGroup.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeGroup&lt;/a&gt;, and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeGroupMembership.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeGroupMembership&lt;/a&gt; to include this information in the backup.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In addition to users and groups, consider backing up account settings such as account customization (the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeAccountCustomization.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeAccountCustomization&lt;/a&gt; API), customized brands (the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeBrand.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeBrand&lt;/a&gt; API), and folders (the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_ListFolders.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ListFolders&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeFolder.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeFolder&lt;/a&gt;, and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeFolderPermissions.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeFolderPermissions&lt;/a&gt; APIs).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Technical implementation&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this section, we cover how to create an automation that orchestrates the invocation of the Quick Sight APIs needed to perform an effective backup implementation. We provide sample code at the end of this section that implements both users and groups backup and Quick Sight assets backup.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="backup-orchestration-flow"&gt;Backup orchestration flow&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The automation tool supports three modes of operation: user backup only, assets backup only, and both. This provides maximum flexibility when you perform your backup plan. The following diagram shows the flow that the tool follows depending on the selected operation mode.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img class="aligncenter" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/05/ML-20472-3.png" alt="Flow diagram of the backup automation tool showing the three operation modes (user backup only, assets backup only, and both) with their orchestration steps" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="users-and-group-backup"&gt;Users and group backup&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The user and groups backup service uses the Quick Sight &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_User.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;user&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_Group.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;group&lt;/a&gt; APIs to read your account’s current state and store the retrieved user and group data in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/dynamodb/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon DynamoDB&lt;/a&gt;. The service uses date-based suffixes for DynamoDB table names to preserve historical backup data and prevent overwrites. This allows point-in-time recovery and backup history tracking. This design also simplifies restore operations because you don’t need to filter by date suffixes when you query data within a specific backup.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img class="aligncenter" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/05/ML-20472-4.png" alt="Diagram of the users and groups backup flow showing how Quick Sight user and group APIs feed three DynamoDB tables: users, groups, and user-group memberships" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Example for a backup run on 2025-10-19:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Users&lt;/strong&gt;: quicksight-users-backup-2025-10-19&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Groups&lt;/strong&gt;: quicksight-groups-backup-2025-10-19&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;User-Group Memberships&lt;/strong&gt;: quicksight-users-groups-backup-2025-10-19&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Users Table Schema:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "user_name": "string (partition key)",
  "arn": "string",
  "email": "string",
  "role": "string",
  "identity_type": "string",
  "active": "boolean",
  "principal_id": "string",
  "backup_timestamp": "string (ISO 8601)",
  "custom_permissions_name": "string"
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Groups Table Schema:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "group_name": "string (partition key)",
  "arn": "string",
  "description": "string",
  "principal_id": "string",
  "members": ["list of user names"],
  "backup_timestamp": "string (ISO 8601)"
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Users-Groups Membership Table Schema:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "membership_id": "string (partition key, format: username#groupname)",
  "user_name": "string",
  "group_name": "string",
  "user_arn": "string",
  "group_arn": "string",
  "backup_timestamp": "string (ISO 8601)"
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; The user and group backup service implements dual-Region support. User and group operations use the &lt;code&gt;identity_region&lt;/code&gt; configuration parameter, while backup asset operations use the standard &lt;code&gt;aws_region&lt;/code&gt;. This design addresses enterprise scenarios where Quick Sight identity management is configured in a different Region than asset storage.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="assets-backup"&gt;Assets backup&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The assets bundle backup service coordinates the export of assets within a Region and uploads the generated bundle to an Amazon S3 location for later use. The automation backs up the following assets: data sources, datasets, analyses, dashboards, and themes. By default, the backup includes all dependencies. You can disable this setting if needed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;At a high level, the service performs the following tasks:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Lists all data sources using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_ListDataSources.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ListDataSources&lt;/a&gt; API, filtering out Amazon S3 manifest-based data sources and data sources with invalid VPC connection names. Names must contain only alphanumeric characters separated by hyphens.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Lists all datasets using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_ListDataSets.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ListDataSets&lt;/a&gt; API, filtering out &lt;code&gt;FILE&lt;/code&gt; datasets by checking the &lt;code&gt;ImportMode&lt;/code&gt; field.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Lists all analyses using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_ListAnalyses.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ListAnalyses&lt;/a&gt; API.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Lists all dashboards using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_ListDashboards.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ListDashboards&lt;/a&gt; API.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Groups assets by type for separate export jobs. You can configure the number of assets to include in each bundle, with a maximum of 100 (the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_StartAssetBundleExportJob.html#QS-StartAssetBundleExportJob-request-ResourceArns" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;API limit&lt;/a&gt;).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Checks the export job status using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksight/latest/APIReference/API_DescribeAssetBundleExportJob.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DescribeAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/a&gt; API and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/prescriptive-guidance/latest/cloud-design-patterns/retry-backoff.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;implements exponential backoff&lt;/a&gt; to avoid throttling.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Uploads the completed asset bundle to Amazon S3 using the following prefix structure.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;my-QuickSight-backups/
└── QuickSight-backups/                          # Custom S3 prefix
    ├── 2024/01/15/
    │   ├── datasources/
    │   │   ├── datasources-143022.zip            # Single bundle (≤ max_assets_per_bundle)
    │   │   └── datasources_bundle_1-143045.zip   # Multiple bundles when assets exceed limit
    │   ├── datasets/
    │   │   ├── datasets_bundle_1-143045.zip      # Multiple bundles when assets exceed limit
    │   │   └── datasets_bundle_2-143045.zip      # Sequential numbering for multiple bundles
    │   ├── analyses/
    │   │   └── analyses-143108.zip               # Single bundle
    │   └── dashboards/
    │       ├── dashboards_bundle_1-143131.zip    # First of multiple dashboard bundles
    │       └── dashboards_bundle_2-143131.zip    # Second dashboard bundle
    └── 2024/01/16/
        ├── datasources/
        │   └── datasources-090015.zip
        ├── datasets/
        │   └── datasets-090030.zip
        └── ...&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; The bundle number string is present only when the number of assets to back up exceeds the configured value in &lt;code&gt;max_assets_per_bundle&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="end-to-end-tool-for-backup-creation"&gt;End-to-end tool for backup creation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The QuickSight-backup tool provides a simple way to export all your Quick Sight assets and their dependencies into durable, inexpensive storage such as Amazon S3. The tool creates new prefixes for generated bundles, so previous backups aren’t overwritten. The tool also exports users and groups using the same principle: DynamoDB stores this data, and table names contain the date when the backup was generated. With this approach, you can use backups as a source for your recovery strategy and track the history of changes to your Quick Sight assets and associated users.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The code uses the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sdk-for-python/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Boto3 Python SDK&lt;/a&gt; and includes packaging support through &lt;a href="https://packaging.python.org/en/latest/guides/distributing-packages-using-setuptools/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;setuptools&lt;/a&gt; for setup and use.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="tooling-usage-and-configuration"&gt;Tooling usage and configuration&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before using the tool, make sure you meet the following prerequisites:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Python 3.8 or higher.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A Quick Sight account with Enterprise edition or higher.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI) configured with appropriate credentials.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Required AWS permissions. See the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-quicksight-backup-tool/blob/main/README.md#permissions" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Permissions section&lt;/a&gt; in the code.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Clone from source&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;git clone https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-quicksight-backup-tool.git
cd quicksight-backup-tool&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Create a Python venv (recommended)&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python3 -m venv ./.venv
source .venv/bin/activate&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Install the package&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;pip install -e .&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Create a configuration file&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started, refer to the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-quicksight-backup-tool/blob/main/examples/config-basic.yaml" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;config-basic.yaml&lt;/a&gt; file in the repo or create one from scratch. This configuration file defines key parameters for the tool, including the following:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS account.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Region.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Backup locations (DynamoDB tables and Amazon S3 bucket prefixes).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Using the tool&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After installation, you can run the tool as follows:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;quicksight-backup --config config.yaml --mode full&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You only need to provide the &lt;code&gt;--config&lt;/code&gt; parameter. You can omit the rest. The &lt;code&gt;--mode&lt;/code&gt; parameter controls the backup type (&lt;code&gt;full&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;users-only&lt;/code&gt;, or &lt;code&gt;assets-only&lt;/code&gt;), where &lt;code&gt;full&lt;/code&gt; is the default mode. The following list describes the arguments the tool supports.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Optional arguments&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--mode&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;-m&lt;/code&gt;: Backup mode (&lt;code&gt;full&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;users-only&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;assets-only&lt;/code&gt;); default is &lt;code&gt;full&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--output-dir&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;-o&lt;/code&gt;: Output directory for reports and manifests.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--verbose&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;-v&lt;/code&gt;: Enable verbose (&lt;code&gt;DEBUG&lt;/code&gt;) logging.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--log-file&lt;/code&gt;: Path to log file.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--dry-run&lt;/code&gt;: Validate configuration without running the backup.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--no-progress&lt;/code&gt;: Disable progress indicators.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--generate-manifest&lt;/code&gt;: Generate a backup manifest file.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--generate-report&lt;/code&gt;: Generate a human-readable backup report.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;--version&lt;/code&gt;: Show version information.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more information, see the tool &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-quicksight-backup-tool/blob/main/README.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;README file&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="tool-code"&gt;Tool code&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can find the code for this tool in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-quicksight-backup-tool" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;aws-samples repository&lt;/a&gt;. This tool helps you get started quickly. Use it as a foundational reference to refine and adapt for your specific backup requirements.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before you implement a backup solution in your production environment, confirm that you:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Review and adapt the code&lt;/strong&gt; to align with your specific infrastructure requirements, security policies, and compliance standards.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Conduct thorough testing&lt;/strong&gt; in a non-production environment to validate functionality and performance.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Implement appropriate security controls&lt;/strong&gt; including encryption, access management, and audit logging required by your organization.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Validate recovery procedures&lt;/strong&gt; to confirm your backup strategy meets your defined Recovery Time Objectives (RTO) and Recovery Point Objectives (RPO).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Consider cost optimization&lt;/strong&gt; strategies and monitoring to keep the solution within your operational budget.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Avoid concurrent tool execution:&lt;/strong&gt; This tool relies on the AssetsAsBundle APIs, which have low throttling thresholds. The sample tool is not designed to run multiple instances in parallel within the same AWS account. If multiple teams need to use the tool, consider implementing a concurrency control mechanism (for example, a lock table in DynamoDB or a database-level lock) to prevent concurrent runs that could trigger API throttling.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="scheduled-execution"&gt;Scheduled execution&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The sample tool described in the previous section is designed for on-demand execution and is well suited for getting started or running ad-hoc backups. For a production-grade backup strategy, you might want to automate backup runs on a recurring schedule so that your Quick Sight assets are consistently protected without manual intervention.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This section outlines the high-level architecture for a scheduled, fully automated backup solution. Detailed implementation and code for this architecture are outside the scope of this post.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="architecture-overview"&gt;Architecture overview&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The scheduled execution architecture is built on three AWS managed services that work together to provide a reliable, serverless, and cost-effective automation pipeline:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/eventbridge/latest/userguide/eb-what-is.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon EventBridge&lt;/a&gt; is the scheduler. It triggers the backup workflow at a defined cadence, for example, daily at midnight. EventBridge rules let you define flexible cron-based or rate-based schedules without managing any underlying infrastructure.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/step-functions/latest/dg/welcome.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Step Functions&lt;/a&gt; is the orchestration layer. It coordinates the run of the individual backup steps in the correct sequence. Step Functions provides built-in error handling, retry logic, and execution history, which makes it well suited for long-running workflows that span multiple API calls and asynchronous operations.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/lambda/latest/dg/welcome.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Lambda&lt;/a&gt; implements each individual backup step as an independent, stateless function. Splitting the backup logic across multiple Lambda functions addresses the time constraints inherent in the backup process. Each export job is asynchronous and might take several minutes to finish, depending on the number and size of assets being exported.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="workflow-steps"&gt;Workflow steps&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Because the end-to-end backup process can take a significant amount of time, the automation is decomposed into discrete steps, each implemented by a dedicated Lambda function. AWS Step Functions orchestrates these functions in sequence, passing state between them and handling retries for transient failures. The workflow consists of the following steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Users and groups backup:&lt;/strong&gt; Retrieves all Quick Sight users, groups, and group memberships using the Quick Sight identity APIs and persists the data to DynamoDB with date-based table suffixes, as described in the &lt;em&gt;Technical implementation&lt;/em&gt; section. This operation can run in parallel with the asset backup operations because it doesn’t have any dependency.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Asset backup discovery:&lt;/strong&gt; Lists all Quick Sight assets in the target Region (data sources, datasets, analyses, and dashboards), applies the necessary filters to exclude unsupported asset types, and groups assets into lists of up to 100 items each. The output of this step is passed to subsequent steps as input.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Generate bundle:&lt;/strong&gt; Initiates export jobs for all the assets included in the list specified as the input parameter, polls for job completion, and uploads the resulting ZIP bundles to the designated Amazon S3 prefix.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Check status:&lt;/strong&gt; Periodically polls the active bundle execution and notifies the AWS Step Functions state machine when the export finishes.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following diagram illustrates the high-level flow of the scheduled execution architecture.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img class="aligncenter" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/05/ML-20472-5.png" alt="Diagram showing scheduled execution architecture with EventBridge triggering a Step Functions state machine that orchestrates Lambda functions for users and groups backup, asset backup discovery, generate bundle, and check status, with bundles uploaded to Amazon S3 and metadata stored in DynamoDB" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="key-design-considerations"&gt;Key design considerations&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Asynchronous polling:&lt;/strong&gt; The check-status Lambda function polls the job initiated by the generate-bundle Lambda function using the &lt;code&gt;DescribeAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/code&gt; API until the job reaches a terminal state (&lt;code&gt;SUCCESSFUL&lt;/code&gt; or &lt;code&gt;FAILED&lt;/code&gt;). The check-status Lambda function runs in a loop with a waiting condition (for example, 30 seconds) between calls.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Parallelism:&lt;/strong&gt; Configure an adequate level of parallelism to control the volume of API calls performed by the steps in your workflow, especially on the generate-bundle step that calls the &lt;code&gt;DescribeAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;StartAssetBundleExportJob&lt;/code&gt; APIs, which have low concurrent rate limits. You can use the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/step-functions/latest/dg/state-map-inline.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;inline map state MaxConcurrency field&lt;/a&gt; to limit the number of concurrent runs of the generate-bundle step.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Error handling:&lt;/strong&gt; Step Functions lets you define catch blocks and retry policies at each stage. A failure in one step (for example, an unsupported asset type) doesn’t abort the entire backup run.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cost:&lt;/strong&gt; When scheduling is enabled, costs scale with backup frequency and retention period. For guidance on estimating storage costs, see the &lt;em&gt;Cost estimation&lt;/em&gt; section.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Cost estimation&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following sections estimate the costs of running the backup tool on Amazon S3 (for asset bundles) and DynamoDB (for user and group metadata).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="amazon-s3-asset-bundle-storage"&gt;Amazon S3: asset bundle storage&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Asset bundles are compressed ZIP files uploaded to Amazon S3 after each export job. Based on the solution design, each bundle of up to 100 assets averages approximately 500 KB when compressed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Key takeaway:&lt;/strong&gt; Amazon S3 storage costs for asset bundles are minimal. Even for very large Quick Sight deployments with thousands of assets, the compressed bundle size remains in the low megabytes range, resulting in a monthly storage cost well below $0.01.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="amazon-dynamodb-user-and-group-metadata-storage"&gt;Amazon DynamoDB: user and group metadata storage&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;User and group information is stored in DynamoDB tables with date-based suffixes to preserve backup history. DynamoDB storage is priced at approximately $0.25 per GB per month (Standard table class, on-demand mode).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each item stored in DynamoDB represents a single user or group definition (including all associated attributes such as ARN, email, role, group memberships, and backup timestamp). Based on the schema described in this post, the average item size is approximately 256 KB.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can use this formula to estimate the size of your DynamoDB tables:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Table size estimate = Number of items × Average item size (256 KB)&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Key takeaway:&lt;/strong&gt; For small and medium organizations, DynamoDB storage costs remain minimal (under $0.10 per month per backup snapshot). For large organizations with tens of thousands of users, costs are still modest, in the low single-digit dollar range per snapshot.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="summary"&gt;Summary&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For a single, unscheduled backup run, the total AWS cost is effectively near zero, dominated by a few cents of Amazon S3 and DynamoDB storage at most. If you implement scheduled backups (covered in the &lt;em&gt;Scheduled execution&lt;/em&gt; section), costs scale linearly with backup frequency and retention period. Even with daily backups retained for 90 days, total storage costs remain in the low single-digit dollar range for most deployments. Consider using Amazon S3 Lifecycle policies and DynamoDB Standard-IA to optimize costs as your backup history grows.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we covered how to design and implement a comprehensive backup strategy for Amazon Quick Sight assets so you can maintain business continuity, meet regulatory requirements, and protect against data loss.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We covered how to use AssetsAsBundle APIs to programmatically export and preserve critical BI assets, including dashboards, analyses, datasets, and data sources, along with their dependencies and permissions. To help you get started, this post includes a sample automation tool that you can test and adapt to your organization’s needs. The code orchestrates these APIs, stores asset bundles in Amazon S3, and preserves user and group information in DynamoDB for point-in-time recovery.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Ready to protect your Quick Sight BI assets? Get started today by cloning the sample backup tool from the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-quicksight-backup-tool" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Samples repository&lt;/a&gt; and testing it in your non-production environment. Begin with a simple configuration to back up your most critical dashboards, then expand to a production-ready backup strategy as you validate the process. To learn more about Amazon Quick Sight, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quicksuite/latest/userguide/welcome.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick Sight User Guide&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;hr&gt;

		<title>Pair Nova 2 Lite with Claude for cost-optimized document processing</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/pair-nova-2-lite-with-claude-for-cost-optimized-document-processing/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Sanghwa Na]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 29 Jun 2026 17:52:33 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Nova]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Textract]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">3a48407f30718965ac0a24f9004a09479b0671ab</guid>

					<description>In this post, we show how pairing Amazon Nova 2 Lite with Anthropic’s Claude Sonnet 4.6 delivers an efficient solution for digitizing scanned documents at scale. We built a two-model pipeline on Amazon Bedrock for digitizing scanned yearbook pages. Amazon Nova 2 Lite handles native multimodal extraction in a single call: detecting photos, extracting visible names with coordinates, and returning page-level metadata. Claude Sonnet 4.6 then performs spatial reasoning to match names to faces based on page layout.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;A scanned yearbook page contains 176 printed names, 4 portrait photographs, and zero machine-readable structure linking them. To digitize this page, you need reliable photo detection with bounding boxes and accurate name extraction. You also need a way to determine which name belongs to which face based on page layout.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we show how pairing Amazon Nova 2 Lite with Anthropic’s Claude Sonnet 4.6 delivers an efficient solution for digitizing scanned documents at scale. We built a two-model pipeline on Amazon Bedrock for digitizing scanned yearbook pages. Amazon Nova 2 Lite handles native multimodal extraction in a single call: detecting photos, extracting visible names with coordinates, and returning page-level metadata. Claude Sonnet 4.6 then performs spatial reasoning to match names to faces based on page layout.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We ran this pipeline against 336 scanned yearbook pages and produced 3,122 name-to-face associations, with 93 percent scoring at or above 0.95 confidence. This two-model approach costs about two-thirds less per page than a single-model alternative that sends the entire task to one vision-language model. See the Cost considerations section for the detailed breakdown.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The pipeline has two stages. Each stage uses a different model, chosen for the specific task it performs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/19/ML-20681-1.png" alt="Architecture diagram of the two-stage yearbook document processing pipeline on Amazon Bedrock: a scanned page image flows into stage 1 (Amazon Nova 2 Lite for photo detection and name extraction), then into stage 2 (Claude Sonnet 4.6 for spatial reasoning with adaptive thinking), producing structured JSON of name-to-face associations." width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Figure 1.&lt;/strong&gt; Two-model pipeline architecture. The scanned page image flows through two sequential stages. In stage 1, Amazon Nova 2 Lite performs native multimodal extraction in a single API call. It detects and classifies photos with bounding boxes, reads visible names on the page and returns their approximate positions, and emits page-level metadata. In stage 2, Claude Sonnet 4.6 performs spatial reasoning to match names to faces using the combined Nova output.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Amazon Nova 2 Lite&lt;/strong&gt; runs first. Because it handles interleaved text and images natively, a single Converse call returns three things:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The detected photos with bounding boxes and classifications.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The names visible on the page with approximate positions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Page-level metadata like titles and categories.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We set reasoning to &lt;strong&gt;LOW&lt;/strong&gt; for this task by including a reasoning configuration in the Converse API call. See the reasoning block in the Step 1 code that follows. Testing across all 336 pages showed no meaningful accuracy difference between &lt;strong&gt;LOW&lt;/strong&gt;, &lt;strong&gt;MEDIUM&lt;/strong&gt;, and &lt;strong&gt;HIGH&lt;/strong&gt; reasoning levels for this structured extraction, and &lt;strong&gt;LOW&lt;/strong&gt; is the cheapest option. Nova exposes this setting through the &lt;code&gt;reasoning_config&lt;/code&gt; field. Claude, in Step 2, uses a separate &lt;code&gt;thinking&lt;/code&gt; field, so the two models control reasoning under different names.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Asking &lt;em&gt;Nova 2 Lite&lt;/em&gt; only for names, not every OCR token on the page, is what keeps the first stage cheap. The downstream spatial reasoning step doesn’t need the full text of class rosters or event descriptions. It needs names that appear near photos. Constraining Nova output to names keeps the output-token cost at approximately 1,000 tokens per page instead of the approximated 4,500 tokens a full OCR pass would produce.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Claude Sonnet 4.6 enters only at stage 2 for the spatial reasoning step. Given Nova names-with-positions and photo bounding boxes, Claude determines which names correspond to which faces. This step requires handling page layout variability, because yearbook layouts vary from page to page. Captions might appear above or below photos, and some pages mix portrait grids with group shots. Claude adaptive thinking handles this variability without additional prompt engineering per layout type.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this solution, Nova 2 Lite handles the high-volume extraction work natively, in one call. Claude is called once per page for the spatial reasoning step.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="nova-2-lite-fixed-per-image-pricing-predictable-cost-at-scale"&gt;Nova 2 Lite fixed per-image pricing: Predictable cost at scale&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A recent change to how Amazon Nova 2 Lite bills image inputs makes per-page cost predictable at scale, which matters when you are processing hundreds of thousands of pages.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Fixed per-image pricing:&lt;/strong&gt; Amazon Nova 2 Lite bills image and document page inputs at &lt;em&gt;a fixed per-image rate&lt;/em&gt;, regardless of resolution or file size.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This change is significant for document processing pipelines. Previously, image token costs varied based on resolution, making it difficult to project per-page costs without running a proof-of-concept on representative samples. With fixed billing, every image Nova 2 Lite processes is billed at the same per-image rate, regardless of resolution.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For a full page extraction including prompt and output, the per-page cost breaks down as follows:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Component&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tokens/Page&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Rate&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cost/Page&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Image tokens (fixed)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;230&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;$0.30/M input&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;$0.000069&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Prompt tokens (estimated)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;500&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;$0.30/M input&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;$0.000150&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Output tokens (estimated)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;1,000&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;$2.50/M output&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;$0.0025&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Total&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td style="text-align: center;vertical-align: middle" colspan="3"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;~$0.0027&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;At published Nova 2 Lite input-token rates, image input is a small fraction of total per-page cost. For current rates, see the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock pricing page&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For yearbook-scale workloads (hundreds of thousands of pages annually), this fixed pricing makes cost forecasting straightforward because image input cost scales linearly with page count and is independent of page resolution. No resolution normalization is required.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="adaptive-thinking-for-spatial-reasoning"&gt;Adaptive thinking for spatial reasoning&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Claude on Amazon Bedrock supports adaptive thinking, a feature where the model decides how much internal reasoning to apply based on the input complexity. You enable it by setting &lt;code&gt;type&lt;/code&gt; to &lt;code&gt;adaptive&lt;/code&gt; in the &lt;code&gt;thinking&lt;/code&gt; configuration of the Converse API:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;response = bedrock_runtime.converse(
    modelId='us.anthropic.claude-sonnet-4-6',
    messages=[{
        'role': 'user',
        'content': [
            {'image': {'format': 'jpeg', 'source': {'bytes': image_bytes}}},
            {'text': spatial_reasoning_prompt}
        ]
    }],
    additionalModelRequestFields={
        'thinking': {
            'type': 'adaptive'
        }
    }
)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With adaptive thinking enabled, Claude adjusts its reasoning depth based on what it receives. A straightforward portrait grid with eight names neatly arranged above eight faces gets a direct response with minimal reasoning. A page where three group photos share a caption block and names appear in a sidebar triggers step-by-step spatial analysis.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In our 336-page run, Claude used extended reasoning on every page, with reasoning traces ranging from 544 to 1,658 characters. Even the simpler pages benefited from some spatial analysis because yearbook layouts are rarely perfectly uniform. The reasoning traces show Claude working through column alignment and vertical offsets between name positions and face positions, and checking caption proximity when group photos appear on the page.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For this type of structured spatial task, adaptive thinking gives you the right amount of reasoning per page without manual tuning. You don’t need to set a fixed token budget or write layout-specific prompts. The model reads the inputs and decides.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cost note on adaptive thinking:&lt;/strong&gt; When adaptive thinking is enabled, keep three cost factors in mind.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Reasoning tokens are billed as output tokens at the standard output rate ($15.00/M output tokens for Claude Sonnet 4.6 through cross-Region inference).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Reasoning traces are returned in the API response under a separate &lt;code&gt;thinking&lt;/code&gt; content block, but aren’t shown to end users.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Monitor &lt;code&gt;inputTokens&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;outputTokens&lt;/code&gt; in the response metadata to track actual cost per page, because reasoning can significantly increase output token counts on complex pages.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="implementation-walkthrough"&gt;Implementation walkthrough&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The full source code, sample images, and Jupyter notebook are available in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/amazon-nova-samples/tree/main/multimodal-understanding/repeatable-patterns/35-hybrid-vision-spatial-reasoning" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Samples repository on GitHub&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before running the pipeline, make sure that you have the following in place:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS account with access to Amazon Bedrock in an AWS Region where Amazon Nova 2 Lite and Claude Sonnet 4.6 are available.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Model access enabled in the Amazon Bedrock console for both &lt;code&gt;us.amazon.nova-2-lite-v1:0&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;us.anthropic.claude-sonnet-4-6&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) principal with permission to call &lt;code&gt;bedrock:InvokeModel&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;bedrock:Converse&lt;/code&gt; on the preceding two models.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Python 3.10 or later with the &lt;code&gt;boto3&lt;/code&gt; SDK installed. The sample notebook also uses &lt;code&gt;rapidfuzz&lt;/code&gt; for fuzzy name matching and Pillow for visualization overlays.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Scanned page images (JPEG or PNG). For image input through the Converse API, image bytes are passed inline in the request.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-1-detect-photos-and-extract-names-with-amazon-nova-2-lite"&gt;Step 1: Detect photos and extract names with Amazon Nova 2 Lite&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Send the scanned page to Amazon Nova 2 Lite with a prompt that requests both detected photos (with bounding boxes and classifications) and visible names (with approximate positions on the page). Nova native multimodal understanding returns both in a single Converse call.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Nova returns bounding boxes on a 0–1000 coordinate scale for both photos and names. Pass both directly into Step 2. Claude reads the same coordinate space when given in the prompt, so no conversion is needed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;def extract_photos_and_names(image_bytes):
    """Detect photos and extract visible names with Amazon Nova 2 Lite."""
    # Using the Converse API consistently for all Bedrock calls

    response = bedrock_runtime.converse(
        modelId='us.amazon.nova-2-lite-v1:0',
        # Note: cross-region inference profile (us.amazon.nova-2-lite-v1:0)
        messages=[{
            'role': 'user',
            'content': [
                {
                    'image': {
                        'format': 'jpeg',
                        'source': {'bytes': image_bytes}
                    }
                },
                {'text': PHOTO_AND_NAME_EXTRACTION_PROMPT}
            ]
        }],
        inferenceConfig={
            'maxTokens': 8000,
            'temperature': 0
        },
        additionalModelRequestFields={
            'reasoning_config': {
                'type': 'enabled',
                'level': 'LOW'
            }
        }
    )

    raw = response['output']['message']['content'][0]['text']
    return json.loads(raw)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The prompt instructs Nova to return a JSON object with both the photos and the names visible on the page.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "page_title": "Junior Class Officers",
  "photos": [
    {
      "bbox": [245, 180, 410, 520],
      "type": "portrait",
      "category": "class_officers",
      "summary": "Individual portrait photo"
    }
  ],
  "names": [
    {
      "text": "Cecilia Phillips",
      "bbox": [260, 540, 395, 570]
    },
    {
      "text": "John Kolander",
      "bbox": [420, 540, 555, 570]
    }
  ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each photo gets a bounding box, a type (portrait, group, or candid), a category tag, and a short description. Each name gets its visible text and its bounding box on the page. The &lt;code&gt;page_title&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;category&lt;/code&gt; fields also serve a second use case: metadata extraction. With one API call, Nova 2 Lite gives you photo detection, names-with-positions for the matching pipeline, and structured metadata. You can use this metadata for search indexing, filtering by event type, or building a table of contents across hundreds of pages.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-2-match-names-to-faces-with-claude"&gt;Step 2: Match names to faces with Claude&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Now pass Nova names-with-positions and photo bounding boxes to Claude for spatial reasoning. Both use the same 0–1000 coordinate space, so no normalization is needed:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;spatial_prompt = f"""Given these names with page coordinates:
{json.dumps(ocr_tokens)}

And these detected photos with bounding boxes:
{json.dumps(photo_detections)}

Match each person's name to their photo based on spatial position.
Return JSON: {{"associations": [{{"name": str, "face_idx": int,
"confidence": float, "reasoning": str}}]}}"""

response = bedrock_runtime.converse(
    modelId='us.anthropic.claude-sonnet-4-6',
    messages=[{
        'role': 'user',
        'content': [
            {'image': {'format': 'jpeg', 'source': {'bytes': image_bytes}}},
            {'text': spatial_prompt}
        ]
    }],
    additionalModelRequestFields={
        'thinking': {'type': 'adaptive'}
    }
)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;On page 50 of our test set, Nova returned 176 name entries and 4 photo bounding boxes. Most of those names are roster and body text elsewhere on the page. Only the names adjacent to the 4 photos are matchable, so Claude produced 5 associations:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "associations": [
    {"name": "Cecilia Phillips", "face_idx": 0, "confidence": 0.95,
     "reasoning": "Row 0, position 1 of 3 - matches caption above photo"},
    {"name": "John Kolander", "face_idx": 1, "confidence": 0.95,
     "reasoning": "Row 0, position 2 of 3 - matches caption above photo"},
    {"name": "Julie Ostrander", "face_idx": 2, "confidence": 0.95,
     "reasoning": "Row 0, position 3 of 3 - matches caption above photo"}
  ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each association includes a reasoning string that explains the spatial logic. This is useful for debugging pages where associations fail.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-3-validate-and-assemble-results"&gt;Step 3: Validate and assemble results&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The final step applies confidence thresholds and fuzzy name matching (using rapidfuzz) to filter out low-quality associations. The pipeline writes two outputs per page: a JSON file with the association data and a visualization image showing lines drawn between matched names and faces.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="results"&gt;Results&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We processed 336 scanned yearbook pages through this pipeline. The pipeline produced 3,122 name-to-face associations total, with 93.3 percent of those associations scoring confidence at or above 0.95. Only 0.3 percent fell below the 0.90 confidence threshold.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/19/ML-20681-2.png" alt="Confidence score distribution histogram showing 2,912 associations at or above 0.95 confidence, 202 between 0.90 and 0.94, and 8 below 0.90." width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Figure 2.&lt;/strong&gt; Confidence score distribution across the 3,122 name-to-face associations produced from 336 scanned yearbook pages. The distribution is heavily skewed toward high confidence: 2,912 associations (93.3 percent) scored at or above 0.95, 202 (6.5 percent) scored between 0.90 and 0.94, and only 8 (0.3 percent) fell below 0.90. Claude Sonnet 4.6 produced confidence scores during the spatial reasoning step. They reflect the model’s certainty that a given name token maps to a specific face bounding box.&lt;br&gt; &lt;img loading="lazy" class="alignnone size-full wp-image-134071" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20681-combined.png" alt="" width="1200" height="549"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Portrait grid pages&lt;/strong&gt; (282 of 336 pages) averaged 10.9 associations per page. These pages have a regular layout where names appear directly above or below corresponding photos, and the pipeline handled them reliably.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Text-only pages&lt;/strong&gt; (class rosters, event descriptions, index pages) had no photos to detect and were correctly skipped.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Mixed-layout pages&lt;/strong&gt; with portraits and group shots on the same page produced partial associations. The pipeline matched names to portrait photos but left group shots unmatched when captions were ambiguous.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The pipeline outputs a JSON report and a visualization for each page. The visualization draws colored lines from each name token to its matched face, which makes it easy to spot errors during manual review.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="cost-considerations"&gt;Cost considerations&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The two-model split means that you pay Nova 2 Lite rates for the combined extraction call (photos, names-with-positions, metadata) and Claude rates for one reasoning call per page. Under Nova 2 Lite’s fixed per-image pricing, the first stage cost is predictable and independent of input resolution. Claude reasoning step dominates per-page cost.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Per-page cost breakdown&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Stage&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Service&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cost Driver&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Approximate Cost/Page&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Photo + name extraction&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Amazon Nova 2 Lite&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Fixed 230 image tokens + ~500 prompt + ~1,000 output&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~$0.0027&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Spatial reasoning&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Claude Sonnet 4.6&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Image + Nova JSON + adaptive reasoning tokens&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~$0.030&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Pipeline total&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td style="text-align: center;vertical-align: middle" colspan="3"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;~$0.033&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following table compares the pipeline against a single-model approach where each page is sent to Claude to do all three tasks (OCR, photo detection, and spatial matching) in one call.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Dimension&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Two-model pipeline&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Single-model Claude&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Input tokens&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Image (Nova: 230 fixed) + Nova JSON → Claude&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Image @ ~1,500 tokens + prompt @ ~1,300 = ~2,800 tokens @ $3/M = ~$0.008&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Output tokens&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Nova: ~1,000 names-JSON; Claude reasoning: ~1,700 @ $15/M = ~$0.026&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~6,000 tokens @ $15/M = ~$0.09&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Cost per page&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~$0.033&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~$0.10&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Cost per 100K pages&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~$3,300&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~$9,800&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;OCR quality&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Nova native multimodal (no separate OCR service)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;General vision-language model&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Tunability&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Swap or tune each stage independently&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Monolithic prompt; all-or-nothing changes&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;At 100,000 pages, that’s a ~$6,500 difference. Beyond cost, the split pipeline also gives you the ability to swap or tune each stage independently without touching the others. For example, you can upgrade only the reasoning model when a better Claude tier ships.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Exact per-page cost depends on three factors:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Your Amazon Bedrock pricing tier.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The number of names Nova emits (scales with page density).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The number of reasoning tokens Claude uses (scales with layout complexity).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For a rough estimate, add one Nova 2 Lite Converse call with a single image input and a names-and-photos prompt. Then add one Claude Converse call where the input includes the image plus Nova JSON output. Check Amazon Bedrock pricing for current rates.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Actual cost for our 336-page test run&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Based on our pipeline run (336 pages, average 10.9 associations per page):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table style="height: 200px" border="1px" width="869" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Component&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Estimated Cost&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Nova 2 Lite photo + name extraction (336 pages × $0.0027)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~$0.91&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Claude spatial reasoning (336 pages × $0.030)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~$10.08&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Total&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;~$10.99 for 336 pages (~$0.033/page)&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Additional cost optimization levers&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For high-volume or non-real-time workloads:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Amazon Bedrock batch inference&lt;/strong&gt;: 50 percent discount on Nova 2 Lite and Claude calls for workloads that can be processed overnight. Submit a JSONL file of requests to Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3), let Amazon Bedrock process asynchronously, and read results the next morning. At batch pricing, the Nova 2 Lite combined stage drops from ~$0.0027 to ~$0.0014 per page.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Prompt caching&lt;/strong&gt;: If you use the same photo detection prompt across thousands of pages (as this pipeline does), prompt caching can reduce cached prompt token costs by up to 90 percent.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Reasoning budget control&lt;/strong&gt;: For Claude, you can set a &lt;code&gt;budgetTokens&lt;/code&gt; cap on &lt;code&gt;thinking&lt;/code&gt; to limit reasoning token costs on simpler pages while still allowing deeper reasoning when needed.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="latency"&gt;Latency&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each page goes through two sequential API calls, so total latency per page is the sum of both. In our test run, the Claude spatial reasoning step took the longest at roughly 20–30 seconds per page because of the image input and adaptive reasoning. Nova 2 Lite completed in a few seconds. For batch processing, you can parallelize across pages because each page is independent.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table style="height: 186px" border="1px" width="793" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Stage&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Typical Latency&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Nova 2 Lite (photos + names extraction)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;2–5 seconds&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Claude spatial reasoning (adaptive thinking)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;20–30 seconds&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Total per page&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~22–35 seconds&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This pipeline uses serverless AWS services with no persistent infrastructure to manage:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Amazon Bedrock&lt;/strong&gt;: Pay-per-call for Nova 2 Lite and Claude, with no provisioned endpoints to delete.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/strong&gt;: If you uploaded yearbook images to Amazon S3 for processing, delete the bucket or objects when finished. Amazon S3 deletion is permanent and irreversible. Confirm you have backups or no longer need the data before deleting.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;IAM roles&lt;/strong&gt;: Delete any roles created specifically for this pipeline (Amazon Bedrock invoke permissions for Nova 2 Lite and Claude Sonnet 4.6).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we showed how splitting document processing across two models on Amazon Bedrock produced 3,122 name-to-face associations across 336 yearbook pages, with 93 percent at high confidence. Amazon Nova 2 Lite handled photo detection and name extraction in a single native-multimodal call. Claude Sonnet 4.6 handled spatial reasoning with adaptive thinking to match names to the right faces.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This pattern applies beyond yearbooks. Any document with photos and associated text (historical archives, personnel directories, real estate listings, product catalogs) needs the same capabilities: detecting visual elements, extracting the relevant text, and connecting the two through spatial reasoning. The solution we describe in this post combines photo detection, text extraction, and spatial reasoning to deliver both cost efficiency and high accuracy.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The full notebook and sample outputs are available in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/amazon-nova-samples/tree/main/multimodal-understanding/repeatable-patterns/35-hybrid-vision-spatial-reasoning" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Samples repository on GitHub&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To learn more about the services used in this post, see the following resources:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/nova/latest/userguide/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova on Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/claude-messages-adaptive-thinking.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Adaptive thinking with Claude on Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt;
