		<item>
		<title>Debugging production agents with Amazon Bedrock AgentCore Observability</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/debugging-production-agents-with-amazon-bedrock-agentcore-observability/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Joshua Lacy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 29 Jun 2026 17:25:21 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon CloudWatch]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">75ff6f3c82b0059dac1623c26b94c453f2dbffa5</guid>

					<description>In this post, you learn how to debug production agent failures using built-in observability capabilities. We walk through common failure patterns, show how to analyze agent behavior with traces and metrics, and provide structured workflows for resolving issues such as infinite loops and tool invocation failures. This is Part 1 of a two-part series. Part 2 covers performance optimization and memory management.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Production artificial intelligence (AI) agents can fail silently. They may return plausible but incorrect answers, enter infinite reasoning loops, or select the wrong tools without triggering error alerts. These failures make debugging production agent behavior difficult because standard logs and metrics do not capture how decisions are made.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonCloudWatch/latest/monitoring/AgentCore-Agents.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Bedrock AgentCore Observability&lt;/a&gt; addresses these debugging challenges by giving you visibility into agent execution across three layers: metrics, traces, and structured logs. You can follow each reasoning step, inspect tool invocations, and identify exactly where execution diverges from expectations. This visibility moves you from detecting that a failure occurred to understanding why it happened. You can trace how your agent reasoned, which tools it selected, and where the workflow broke down, even when no explicit error is raised. In this post, you learn how to debug production agent failures using built-in observability capabilities. We walk through common failure patterns, show how to analyze agent behavior with traces and metrics, and provide structured workflows for resolving issues such as infinite loops and tool invocation failures. This is Part 1 of a two-part series. Part 2 covers performance optimization and memory management.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before following the walkthroughs in this post, make sure you have the required access and tools in place.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You need an AWS account with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/agentcore/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore&lt;/a&gt; access turned on, familiarity with &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonCloudWatch/latest/monitoring/CloudWatch_Dashboards.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon CloudWatch dashboards&lt;/a&gt; and basic log querying, and a working understanding of AWS &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/iam/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Identity and Access Management&lt;/a&gt; (IAM) roles and policies. You also need &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonCloudWatch/latest/monitoring/CloudWatch-Transaction-Search.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;CloudWatch Transaction Search&lt;/a&gt; turned on for your account (see the Enabling observability section) and an Amazon Bedrock AgentCore agent already deployed or permission to deploy one.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="understanding-agent-failure-patterns"&gt;Understanding agent failure patterns&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AI agents fail differently than traditional applications. You might see successful executions that still return incorrect answers, incomplete workflows, or unexpected tool usage. These issues often appear in production without triggering standard error alerts, which makes them harder to detect and diagnose. Most production issues fall into three categories: quality, reliability, and efficiency. Understanding these patterns helps you narrow your investigation quickly.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="quality-failures"&gt;Quality failures&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Quality failures occur when your agent completes a task but returns incorrect results. Monitoring systems often show successful executions while users receive inaccurate responses. Hallucinations and factual errors appear frequently. An agent might reference policies that do not exist or generate data to fill gaps. In multi-agent systems, these errors can propagate when one agent’s output becomes another agent’s input. Reasoning issues can also surface. An agent may repeat the same incorrect calculation or choose an inappropriate tool. When this happens, reviewing execution traces helps you identify where the logic breaks down.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="reliability-issues"&gt;Reliability issues&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Reliability issues prevent your agent from completing its workflow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Tool invocation failures are a common cause. Your agent might receive a 401 error because of missing credentials, a 403 error because its role lacks permissions, or a 400 error from invalid input. Each error points to a different root cause.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You may also encounter context loss, where the agent fails to retain session state and treats follow-up requests like new conversations. This usually indicates issues with session management or memory configuration.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="efficiency-problems"&gt;Efficiency problems&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Efficiency problems affect cost and performance rather than correctness. High latency slows response times and reduces user engagement. When responses take too long, users abandon interactions or retry requests. Excessive token usage increases cost without improving results. You may see this when agents generate overly verbose responses, retrieve full documents unnecessarily, or repeat tool calls instead of caching results.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="your-debugging-toolkit"&gt;Your debugging toolkit&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can monitor, trace, and analyze agent behavior through three layers of observability: dashboards for system-level visibility, traces for execution-level detail, and metrics for alerting and trend analysis. These capabilities work together to help you move from detecting an issue to identifying its root cause.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="amazon-cloudwatch-dashboards"&gt;Amazon CloudWatch dashboards&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Monitor agent performance in real time through Amazon CloudWatch metrics including session volume, latency, token usage, and error rates. These metrics give you visibility across your agents, memory systems, and tool integrations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The GenAI Observability dashboard displays session volume, invocation latency, token usage, and error rates in a unified view. You can filter these metrics by agent ID, session ID, or time range to focus on specific performance patterns.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When you spot anomalies, CloudWatch alarms notify you automatically when latency increases beyond acceptable thresholds or error rates spike above normal levels.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="opentelemetry-traces"&gt;OpenTelemetry traces&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Dashboards show system behavior at a high level. Traces show how each request executes step by step.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore emits distributed traces, structured span-level logs, and metrics under the bedrock-agentcore CloudWatch namespace. This telemetry follows the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonCloudWatch/latest/monitoring/CloudWatch-OpenTelemetry-Sections.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;OpenTelemetry&lt;/a&gt; (OTEL) protocol and routes to Amazon CloudWatch by default. If your organization uses Datadog, Grafana Cloud, or Elastic Observability, you can export the same telemetry to those backends without additional instrumentation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each trace captures the complete execution flow: reasoning steps, tool invocations, memory retrievals, and final outputs. This granular visibility pinpoints where execution diverges from expected behavior and shows the sequence of decisions that led to the issue.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="key-metrics-to-monitor"&gt;Key metrics to monitor&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Focus on three metric categories: performance, resource usage, and reliability.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="performance-metrics"&gt;Performance metrics&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Track latency at the 50th, 95th, and 99th percentiles. Higher latency often signals downstream bottlenecks. Measure memory retrieval time and tool response time separately. This helps isolate which component slows execution.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="resource-metrics"&gt;Resource metrics&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Session duration reveals usage patterns. Short sessions may indicate user frustration. Long sessions can signal loops or complex workflows. Concurrent sessions show how many active interactions your system supports. Token usage directly impacts cost. Monitor input and output tokens separately to identify inefficiencies.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="reliability-metrics"&gt;Reliability metrics&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Error rates show how often executions fail.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Break these down by type:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Authentication errors.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Authorization errors.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Validation errors.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Timeout errors.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A spike in any category points to a specific area for investigation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="enabling-observability"&gt;Enabling observability&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before you begin debugging, enable CloudWatch Transaction Search for your account. This allows Amazon Bedrock AgentCore to send trace and metric data to CloudWatch. After you enable this setting, the service begins collecting observability data across your agents, memory systems, and tool integrations. You can then access this data through CloudWatch dashboards and Logs Insights queries.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="step-by-step-troubleshooting-workflows"&gt;Step-by-step troubleshooting workflows&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following scenarios walk you through diagnosing and resolving two of the most common production failures. Each scenario shows the symptoms you observe, the Amazon CloudWatch Logs Insights queries you run (each typically taking 2-3 minutes), and the fixes you implement. CloudWatch Logs Insights is a fully managed query service that lets you search and analyze structured log data from Bedrock AgentCore in real time. You access it through the CloudWatch console under the Logs menu.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="scenario-1-debugging-infinite-agent-loops"&gt;Scenario 1: Debugging infinite agent loops&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Infinite loops occur when agents lack proper termination conditions or fail to recognize when they have made mistakes. Before diving into logs, understanding the three most common root causes will speed up your diagnosis.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="common-causes-of-infinite-loops"&gt;Common causes of infinite loops&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Poor prompt design happens when system prompts do not establish clear termination conditions. The prompt may not specify how many attempts are reasonable, when to declare a task impossible, or when to escalate to a human. Missing loop detection happens when the agent’s reasoning framework does not recognize repeated actions. Without explicit logic to track prior attempts, the agent has no way to detect the pattern “I have tried this three times and it is not working.” Incorrect tool selection happens when the agent consistently picks the wrong tool, such as trying to solve a math problem with a web search tool instead of a calculator.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="symptoms-to-watch-for"&gt;Symptoms to watch for&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When an agent enters a loop, token usage increases significantly. Session duration also grows beyond normal ranges. In some cases, the agent generates multiple responses without user input. Notably, the error rate remains low because the agent is not crashing. It is simply unable to finish its task.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20441-1.png" alt="CloudWatch GenAI Observability dashboard showing 266.9K total tokens consumed with a 0% error rate" width="600" height="357"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;The CloudWatch GenAI Observability Dashboard shows 266.9K total tokens consumed with a 0% error rate. High token usage combined with no errors is the key indicator of an infinite loop — the agent is running but cannot complete its task.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="diagnosing-poor-prompt-engineering"&gt;Diagnosing poor prompt engineering&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start by identifying the problematic session. Run this query in CloudWatch Logs Insights to find sessions with unusually high token usage:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;fields @timestamp, SessionId, TokenUsage
| filter TokenUsage &amp;gt; 10000
| sort TokenUsage desc
| limit 20&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Pick the highest consumer and note its SessionId. Then examine the agent’s reasoning pattern within that session:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;fields @timestamp, @message, RequestId
| filter SessionId = "&amp;lt;SessionId&amp;gt;"
| filter Operation like /InvokeAgent/
| sort @timestamp asc
| limit 1000&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20441-2.png" alt="Session details showing a single trace with 177 spans and 85,590 ms average latency" width="600" height="489"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Session details show a single trace with 177 spans and an average latency of 85,590 ms (~85 seconds). Normal agent responses complete in 1–5 seconds. The span count and execution time together confirm the agent entered a loop.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Look for repeated patterns in the agent’s reasoning. The following log sequence is a clear indicator of a loop:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;"Try using calculator tool with input 25"
"Result: 24.95"
"This is incorrect, try again"
"Try using calculator tool with input 25"
"Result: 24.95"
"This is incorrect, try again"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20441-3.png" alt="OpenTelemetry trace waterfall showing a system prompt with no termination condition that caused the loop" width="600" height="330"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;The OpenTelemetry trace waterfall reveals the root cause: a system prompt instructing the agent to “never give up” and “keep trying until you get the exact answer” with no termination condition. This prompt design flaw directly caused the 177-span loop shown in Figure 2.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To fix prompt design issues, add explicit termination conditions to your agent’s system prompt. Include an instruction such as: “If you attempt the same action three times without success, stop and explain to the user why you cannot complete the task.” Set a maximum token limit per session, typically 5,000 to 10,000 tokens for a conversational agent, and implement reasoning step limits of 10 to 15 steps as a hard stop regardless of internal logic.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="diagnosing-loop-detection-failures"&gt;Diagnosing loop detection failures&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run this query to examine tool invocation sequences:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;fields @timestamp, ToolName, ToolInput, ToolOutput
| filter SessionId = "&amp;lt;SessionId&amp;gt;"
| filter Operation like /InvokeTool/
| sort @timestamp asc&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following pattern in results confirms a loop detection failure:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;2026-02-02 22:02:39 | calculate_percentage | {"value": 25, "total": 100} | 25.0
2026-02-02 22:02:45 | calculate_percentage | {"value": 25, "total": 100} | 25.0
2026-02-02 22:02:51 | calculate_percentage | {"value": 25, "total": 100} | 25.0
[repeated 40 more times]&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20441-4.png" alt="CloudWatch Logs Insights results showing 86 repeated calculate_percentage invocations with near-identical inputs" width="600" height="558"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;CloudWatch Logs Insights results show 86 repeated tool invocations of calculate_percentage with near-identical inputs, returning values like 24.954% and 25.049% — never the exact 25.00% the prompt demanded. The repeated near-miss values in the whole column confirm the loop detection failure.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To fix loop detection failures, add loop detection to your agent framework. Track tool invocations and reasoning steps. Force a termination after three identical repeated actions. Set a CloudWatch alarm to notify you when average token usage per session increases significantly. This catches loop problems early, helping prevent runaway costs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="diagnosing-incorrect-tool-selection"&gt;Diagnosing incorrect tool selection&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Review the agent’s tool selection reasoning in the trace events. The following log pattern indicates the agent is choosing the wrong tool:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;"User wants to calculate 25% of 100"
"I should use the web_search tool to find the answer"
[web_search returns irrelevant results]
"Let me try web_search again with different terms"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To fix tool selection, provide clearer tool descriptions with explicit usage examples in your agent’s configuration:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "tools": [
    {
      "name": "calculator",
      "description": "Use this tool for mathematical calculations, including
      percentages, arithmetic, and numerical operations.
      Example: calculating 25% of 100."
    },
    {
      "name": "web_search",
      "description": "Use this tool to find information on the internet.
      Do NOT use for mathematical calculations."
    }
  ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="scenario-2-tool-invocation-failures"&gt;Scenario 2: Tool invocation failures&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Tool invocation failures generate explicit errors and elevated error rates in your CloudWatch dashboard. Unlike infinite loops, users receive clear failure messages. The challenge is identifying the root cause quickly.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="common-types-of-tool-invocation-failures"&gt;Common types of tool invocation failures&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Five error types account for most tool invocation failures, each pointing to a different fix. Authentication errors (401) occur when credentials are expired, missing, or using the wrong authentication method. Authorization errors (403) arise when the IAM role attached to the agent is missing required policies. Validation errors (400) happen when the agent’s input does not match the tool’s expected schema. Resource not found errors (404) indicate an incorrect tool name, invalid resource ID, or deleted tool. Tool execution errors (500) mean the tool itself failed because of internal errors, timeouts, or rate limits.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="symptoms-to-watch-for-1"&gt;Symptoms to watch for&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Tool invocation failures generate explicit errors. Your CloudWatch dashboard shows elevated error rates, agent sessions fail to complete, and users report that the agent cannot access information or perform actions.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="diagnosing-authentication-and-authorization-errors"&gt;Diagnosing authentication and authorization errors&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start by identifying which tool fails most often. Run this query in CloudWatch Logs Insights:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;fields @timestamp, ToolName, StatusCode, ErrorMessage
| filter Operation like /InvokeTool/
| filter StatusCode like /4[0-9][0-9]|5[0-9][0-9]/
| stats count(*) by ToolName, StatusCode
| sort count desc&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20441-5.png" alt="CloudWatch Logs Insights query breaking down tool errors: Exception 45, PermissionError 6, RuntimeError 6" width="600" height="283"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;A CloudWatch Logs Insights query breaks down tool invocation errors by type: Exception (45 occurrences), PermissionError (6), and RuntimeError (6). Exception errors dominate, pointing to validation failures as the primary root cause to investigate first.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For authentication and authorization errors, examine the Gateway service role attached to your agent’s gateway. In Amazon Bedrock AgentCore, tools are accessed through Gateways, and the Gateway service role must have permissions to invoke downstream resources:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;fields @timestamp, AgentId, ExecutionRoleArn, ErrorMessage
| filter StatusCode = 401 or StatusCode = 403
| limit 100&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Compare the Gateway service role’s policies against the permissions the failing tool requires. For example, if your agent invokes an AWS Lambda function tool through the gateway and receives a 403 error, update the Gateway service role to include the following permissions:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [
    {
      "Sid": "AllowGatewayToInvokeLambdaTool",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "lambda:InvokeFunction"
      ],
      "Resource": [
        "arn:aws:lambda:us-east-1:123456789012:function:my-tool-function"
      ]
    }
  ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For credential issues, use AWS Secrets Manager to store and rotate credentials automatically. Set a CloudWatch alarm for tool error rates. Investigate immediately if any tool’s error rate exceeds 5%.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="diagnosing-validation-errors"&gt;Diagnosing validation errors&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For validation errors, examine the tool input your agent provided:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;fields @timestamp, ToolName, ToolInput, ErrorMessage
| filter StatusCode = 400
| limit 50&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Compare the agent’s input against the tool’s expected schema. Look for missing required fields, incorrect data types, or malformed values:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;Tool expected: {"customer_id": "string", "amount": number}
Agent provided: {"customer_id": 12345, "amount": "100.00"}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To fix validation errors, update your tool schemas to match the tool’s current API. Test tool invocations independently before deploying to production. Create integration tests that invoke each tool with various inputs including edge cases.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="diagnosing-resource-and-execution-errors"&gt;Diagnosing resource and execution errors&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For resource not found errors (404) and tool execution errors (500), check the tool’s own logs and metrics first. The problem may not be in your agent. Query for specific error patterns:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;fields @timestamp, ToolName, ToolInput, ErrorMessage
| filter StatusCode = 404 or StatusCode = 500
| limit 50&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When a tool fails, your agent should log the error with full context including the tool name, input payload, and error message. It should retry with exponential backoff for transient failures, gracefully degrade by attempting alternative approaches when a tool is unavailable, and inform users clearly when a tool cannot be reached.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="from-debugging-to-proactive-monitoring"&gt;From debugging to proactive monitoring&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The queries in the previous scenarios help you diagnose issues &lt;strong&gt;after&lt;/strong&gt; they occur. To catch problems before users report them, convert your diagnostic queries into persistent dashboards and alarms, and use &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/evaluators.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AgentCore Evaluators&lt;/a&gt; for continuous tooling assessment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="creating-a-cloudwatch-alarm-from-logs-insights"&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonCloudWatch/latest/monitoring/cloudwatch-metrics-insights-alarms.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Creating a CloudWatch alarm from Logs Insights&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Metric filters can continuously monitor your agent’s log group for patterns that indicate failures, and by attaching a CloudWatch alarm, your team can be automatically alerted.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This gives you an early warning system for infinite loops without requiring anyone to manually run Logs Insights queries.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="building-a-cloudwatch-dashboard"&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonCloudWatch/latest/logs/CWL_ExportQueryResults.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Building a CloudWatch dashboard&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Add key diagnostic queries as CloudWatch dashboard widgets to maintain ongoing visibility. This gives your operations team a single view of agent health without needing to re-run queries manually. The visualization can help with identifying additional outliers.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="automated-tool-accuracy-with-agentcore-evaluators"&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/evaluations.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Automated tool accuracy with AgentCore Evaluators&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For tool accuracy monitoring at scale, Amazon Bedrock AgentCore Evaluators provide continuous, automated assessment of agent behavior. Rather than manually reviewing traces when something goes wrong, Evaluators inspect agent sessions in real time and score them against quality criteria to evaluate agent performance at scale.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up-resources"&gt;Clean up resources&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After completing the walkthroughs, remove or disable the resources you created to avoid ongoing charges.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To disable CloudWatch queries and alarms, open the Amazon CloudWatch console, navigate to Logs -&amp;gt; Log Insights and delete any saved queries you created, then navigate to Alarms &amp;gt; All Alarms and delete any alarms you created for token usage or error rate thresholds. If you turned on CloudWatch Transaction Search solely for this walkthrough, turn it off in the CloudWatch console under Settings.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; Turning off CloudWatch Transaction Search stops all future trace collection. Make sure you no longer need observability data before turning it off. You can turn it back on at your discretion.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you deployed a test agent for this walkthrough, navigate to the Amazon Bedrock console, select your agent, and choose Delete to remove it and its associated resources.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You now have a practical framework for debugging production agents. You understand the three categories of agent failures: how Bedrock AgentCore’s CloudWatch dashboards and OpenTelemetry traces surface each failure type, and how to follow structured diagnostic workflows to resolve infinite loops and tool invocation failures.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The key takeaway: structured observability turns hours of guesswork into minutes of targeted investigation. The CloudWatch Logs Insights queries in this post are ready to use in your production environment today.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="next-steps"&gt;Next steps&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Continue with Part 2 of this series, “Optimizing Production Agents: Performance and Memory Management with Bedrock AgentCore,” which tackles performance bottlenecks and memory leaks. To build custom queries for your specific agent workflows, explore the Amazon CloudWatch Logs Insights documentation. Share your debugging experience and get help from the community in the AWS Machine Learning Community Forums.&lt;/p&gt;

		<title>Build interactive PDF text extraction from Amazon S3</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/build-interactive-pdf-text-extraction-from-amazon-s3/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Phani Parcha]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Fri, 26 Jun 2026 14:47:45 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Simple Storage Service (S3)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">7c2058ee7f519e8f9d0569ec0096d6fa0be8e649</guid>

					<description>In this post, you’ll build a server that extracts text from PDF files in Amazon S3 in real time. This protocol-based approach provides programmatic document access. You’ll walk through the architecture, set up the server, and run interactive document queries. Along the way, you’ll compare this approach with Amazon Textract so you can decide which tool fits your workload.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Picture this: a compliance officer needs a specific clause during an audit, an attorney needs contract terms while a client waits on the phone, or a finance analyst needs numbers from last quarter’s report before a meeting that starts in 10 minutes. In each case, waiting for a scheduled job to finish is not practical. You need on-demand access to the text inside your PDFs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you’ll build a server that extracts text from PDF files in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/serv-s3/?trk=bdbb278d-6d78-4cd9-9c3b-82aca1fe11a5&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=5fa2d842-4ea7-471c-b68f-6855f38d19ae&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088730!e!!g!!amazon%20s3!23846236262!198027689882&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23846236262&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_vQHXb5TzskukzicH9_9TZB&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; in real time. This protocol-based approach provides programmatic document access. You’ll walk through the architecture, set up the server, and run interactive document queries. Along the way, you’ll compare this approach with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/textract/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt; so you can decide which tool fits your workload.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We built this solution after working with several teams who shared the same frustration: their documents lived in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/serv-s3/?trk=bdbb278d-6d78-4cd9-9c3b-82aca1fe11a5&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=5fa2d842-4ea7-471c-b68f-6855f38d19ae&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088730!e!!g!!amazon%20s3!23846236262!198027689882&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23846236262&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_vQHXb5TzskukzicH9_9TZB&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt;, but getting text out of them on demand meant either writing custom scripts or waiting on batch pipelines. This MCP server approach sits in between, giving you interactive access with minimal setup. Interactive PDF text extraction from &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/serv-s3/?trk=bdbb278d-6d78-4cd9-9c3b-82aca1fe11a5&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=5fa2d842-4ea7-471c-b68f-6855f38d19ae&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088730!e!!g!!amazon%20s3!23846236262!198027689882&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23846236262&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_vQHXb5TzskukzicH9_9TZB&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; gives you real-time answers from your documents without batch pipelines or heavy infrastructure.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This MCP-based option works well for text-based PDFs in development and proof of concept settings. For complex document processing like optical character recognition (OCR), form extraction, and layout analysis, Amazon Textract remains the recommended choice.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="who-benefits-from-this-approach"&gt;Who benefits from this approach&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This solution fits several common roles. If these scenarios sound like your day-to-day, read on.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Compliance and legal teams: During a time-sensitive review, you need to locate a specific clause buried in a 200-page policy document or contract. Searching manually takes too long. With this solution, you ask a question in natural language and get the relevant passage back in seconds.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Financial services teams: During an audit session, you need immediate access to the exact wording of an internal risk policy or regulatory filing. This solution lets you pull that information directly from your &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/serv-s3/?trk=bdbb278d-6d78-4cd9-9c3b-82aca1fe11a5&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=5fa2d842-4ea7-471c-b68f-6855f38d19ae&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088730!e!!g!!amazon%20s3!23846236262!198027689882&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23846236262&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_vQHXb5TzskukzicH9_9TZB&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; document repository without leaving your terminal.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Executive teams: During strategic planning meetings, you can query a PDF on the spot when someone asks about a data point from last quarter’s earnings report. No flipping through printed copies or waiting for someone to look it up after the meeting.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;These scenarios share a few common traits: they involve real-time information needs where batch processing is too slow, text-based PDF documents with standard formatting, cost sensitivity in development and proof of concept environments, and integration requirements with existing AWS workflows and tooling.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="where-this-approach-fits-alongside-amazon-textract"&gt;Where this approach fits alongside Amazon Textract&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/textract/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt; is a fully managed AWS AI service purpose-built for document processing at scale. It handles scanned pages, handwriting, and multi-column layouts. Choose &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/textract/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt; when you need OCR for scanned documents, advanced form and table extraction, complex layout analysis, production-scale batch processing with service level agreement (SLA) requirements, or compliance features and enterprise support.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The MCP-based approach addresses a complementary scenario: giving an AI assistant interactive, on-demand access to text already encoded inside PDFs. Choose this pattern when your documents are text-based PDFs (no OCR required), your workflow is interactive rather than batch, you are working in development or proof of concept environments, and you want minimal infrastructure between the AI assistant and the source document. For everything else, including any document processing that benefits from OCR or structured extraction, route the work to Amazon Textract.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="how-the-solution-works"&gt;How the solution works&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With this solution, you connect your AI assistant directly to your PDF documents in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; and can get answers quickly. Under the hood, the solution uses the Model Context Protocol (MCP), an open standard that provides a structured way to access external data sources. MCP acts as a communication layer between your application and your data. The architecture has four components: a command-line interface as the user interface, the MCP layer for communication, a custom MCP server for PDF processing, and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/serv-s3/?trk=bdbb278d-6d78-4cd9-9c3b-82aca1fe11a5&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=5fa2d842-4ea7-471c-b68f-6855f38d19ae&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088730!e!!g!!amazon%20s3!23846236262!198027689882&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23846236262&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_vQHXb5TzskukzicH9_9TZB&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; for document storage, secured by &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/iam/?trk=6a436c72-0178-4620-97ad-0220ccc59fd0&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=7f76fd5a-1dd3-456d-a5b3-e55003fb8e27&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxj8MoqmOzo8wrxwsp8UrVuxp4qg8F0giXUDG-cf6qJ_0XuYQH7z3NwaAuyzEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088700!e!!g!!amazon%20iam!23840942619!198027689202&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23840942619&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp-JGFhG3yn4F6qwsdkuBx5yF&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxj8MoqmOzo8wrxwsp8UrVuxp4qg8F0giXUDG-cf6qJ_0XuYQH7z3NwaAuyzEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Identity and Access Management (AWS IAM)&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-19835-1.png" alt="Architecture diagram showing PDF text extraction workflow with components including Amazon Q Developer CLI, MCP Protocol Layer, MCP Server with PDF Text Extraction, Amazon S3 for document storage, and Security &amp;amp; Audit Layer with AWS CloudTrail and AWS IAM." width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="cost-comparison"&gt;Cost comparison&lt;/h3&gt;

&lt;p&gt;These two figures are price points for different feature sets and should not be read as a head-to-head price comparison. Use them to pick the right tool for the workload, not to optimize purely on dollars. If your workload involves scanned documents, forms, tables, complex layouts, or production SLAs, Amazon Textract is the appropriate choice and the additional capabilities are reflected in its price.&lt;/p&gt;

&lt;h2 id="architecture-overview"&gt;Architecture overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-19835-2.png" alt="Component diagram showing the S3 PDF MCP Server architecture with Client Environment (User/Client, Kiro CLI, MCP Client) connecting to S3 PDF MCP Server containing StdioServer Transport, S3PdfMcpServer, Tool Handler with Extract s3_pdf_text function, AWS SDK S3 Client, and PDF Parser, all connecting to AWS S3 for PDF document storage." width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following sequence diagram illustrates the end-to-end workflow for extracting text from a PDF stored in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/serv-s3/?trk=bdbb278d-6d78-4cd9-9c3b-82aca1fe11a5&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=5fa2d842-4ea7-471c-b68f-6855f38d19ae&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088730!e!!g!!amazon%20s3!23846236262!198027689882&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23846236262&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_vQHXb5TzskukzicH9_9TZB&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt;. The process begins when the AI client initiates a request for PDF extraction through the CLI. The system forwards this request to the MCP server, which retrieves the PDF file from &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/serv-s3/?trk=bdbb278d-6d78-4cd9-9c3b-82aca1fe11a5&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=5fa2d842-4ea7-471c-b68f-6855f38d19ae&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088730!e!!g!!amazon%20s3!23846236262!198027689882&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23846236262&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_vQHXb5TzskukzicH9_9TZB&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; using the provided bucket and object key.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After the MCP server fetches the PDF, it passes the file to a PDF parsing component. The component processes the document and extracts the textual content. The MCP server then returns the extracted text to the client, and the client displays it to the user.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-19835-3.png" alt="Sequence diagram showing the PDF text extraction flow: AI Client requests PDF extraction from Kiro CLI, which calls extract_s3_pdf_text on MCP Server, MCP Server retrieves PDF from Amazon S3 using GetObject, PDF Parser processes the content and returns extracted text back through the chain to display to the user" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="step-by-step-implementation"&gt;Step-by-step implementation&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Follow these steps to set up and configure the PDF text extraction solution. Begin by confirming you have the required prerequisites in place.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before you begin, confirm that you have the following items ready. You’ll also need basic familiarity with Python programming and AWS services.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS account with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/serv-s3/?trk=bdbb278d-6d78-4cd9-9c3b-82aca1fe11a5&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=5fa2d842-4ea7-471c-b68f-6855f38d19ae&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!808827088730!e!!g!!amazon%20s3!23846236262!198027689882&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23846236262&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_vQHXb5TzskukzicH9_9TZB&amp;amp;gclid=Cj0KCQjwlqTRBhCBARIsANrkrxgL8GfZYjtZLwEX5bw5yBWaDe1Wi-glJBRhTHaYs1iB6rU8b5S19FwaAkVCEALw_wcB" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; read permissions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Python 3.10 or later installed.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI) configured with valid credentials.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://kiro.dev/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Kiro CLI&lt;/a&gt; installed.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt; 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="lang-bash"&gt;pip install boto3 PyPDF2 mcp&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="installation"&gt;Installation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This section guides you through installing the MCP server and its dependencies. The process involves creating a Python virtual environment, installing the required packages, and creating the server file. Follow these steps in order. Run each command in your terminal.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Before you start, you need:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Python 3.10 or newer installed on your machine.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The &lt;a href="https://kiro.dev/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Kiro CLI&lt;/a&gt; installed and logged in.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS credentials set up on your machine (run &lt;code&gt;aws configure&lt;/code&gt; if you haven’t).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An S3 bucket that contains at least one PDF file.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 1 — Create a folder for the project&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run these two commands in your terminal:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;mkdir ~/s3-pdf-extractor&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 2 — Navigate to the project folder&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run this command:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;cd ~/s3-pdf-extractor&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 3 — Create a Python virtual environment&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run this command:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python3 -m venv venv&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 4 — Activate the virtual environment&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run this command:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;source venv/bin/activate&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After this, your terminal prompt will show &lt;code&gt;(venv)&lt;/code&gt; at the start. Keep this terminal open. You need to stay in this virtual environment for the next steps.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 5 — Install the required Python packages&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run this one command:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;pip install mcp boto3 PyPDF2&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Wait for it to finish. It should end with “Successfully installed…”.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 6 — Create the server file&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Inside the &lt;code&gt;~/s3-pdf-extractor&lt;/code&gt; folder, create a new file named &lt;strong&gt;exactly:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;s3_pdf_extractor.py&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Paste the following code into that file and save it:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 7 — Test that the server starts&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In your terminal (still inside the &lt;code&gt;s3-pdf-extractor&lt;/code&gt; folder with the venv active), run:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python s3_pdf_extractor.py&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The terminal will appear to “pause” with no output. That is correct. It means the server is running and waiting for requests. Press &lt;code&gt;Ctrl+C&lt;/code&gt; to stop it.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you see an error instead, re-check Steps 2 and 3.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;from mcp.server import Server
from mcp.types import Tool, TextContent
import boto3
from PyPDF2 import PdfReader
import tempfile
import os
import logging

# Configure logging for production use
logging.basicConfig(level=logging.INFO)
logger = logging.getLogger(__name__)

server = Server("s3-pdf-extractor")

@server.list_tools()
async def list_tools():
    return [
        Tool(
            name="extract_s3_pdf_text",
            description="Extract text content from a PDF stored in Amazon S3",
            inputSchema={
                "type": "object",
                "properties": {
                    "bucket": {"type": "string", "description": "S3 bucket name"},
                    "key": {"type": "string", "description": "S3 object key"}
                },
                "required": ["bucket", "key"]
            }
        )
    ]

@server.call_tool()
async def call_tool(name: str, arguments: dict):
    if name == "extract_s3_pdf_text":
        bucket = arguments["bucket"]
        key = arguments["key"]

        try:
            # Use existing AWS credentials and IAM permissions
            s3_client = boto3.client('s3')

            with tempfile.NamedTemporaryFile(delete=False, suffix='.pdf') as tmp_file:
                s3_client.download_file(bucket, key, tmp_file.name)
                tmp_path = tmp_file.name

            # Extract text using PyPDF2
            reader = PdfReader(tmp_path)
            text = ""
            for page in reader.pages:
                text += page.extract_text() + "\n"

            logger.info(f"Successfully extracted text from {bucket}/{key}")
            return [TextContent(type="text", text=text)]

        except Exception as e:
            logger.error(f"Error processing {bucket}/{key}: {str(e)}")
            raise
        finally:
            # Ensure cleanup of temporary files
            if 'tmp_path' in locals():
                os.unlink(tmp_path)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    server.run()&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 8 — Locate or create the Kiro CLI configuration file&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Kiro CLI uses a JSON configuration file to know which MCP servers are available. You need to add your server to this file.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Kiro CLI MCP configuration file is located at:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;~/.kiro/settings/tools/mcp.json&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If this file does not exist, create it by running these commands in your terminal:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;mkdir -p ~/.kiro/settings/tools
nano ~/.kiro/settings/tools/mcp.json&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 9 — Add the MCP server configuration&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Paste the following JSON into the file. Replace &lt;code&gt;/path/to/s3_pdf_extractor.py&lt;/code&gt; with the actual path from Step 1 (for example, &lt;code&gt;~/s3-pdf-extractor/s3_pdf_extractor.py&lt;/code&gt;):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
    "mcpServers": {
        "s3-pdf-extractor": {
            "command": "python",
            "args": ["/path/to/s3_pdf_extractor.py"]
        }
    }
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get the full absolute path, run &lt;code&gt;echo ~/s3-pdf-extractor/s3_pdf_extractor.py&lt;/code&gt; in your terminal and use that output in the args field.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 10 — Save the configuration file&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Press Ctrl+O, then press Enter to save the file.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 11 — Close the file editor&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Press Ctrl+X to exit nano.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 12 — Restart Kiro CLI&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Restart Kiro CLI to load the new configuration. Close and reopen Kiro CLI, or run:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kiro --restart&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 13 — Verify the MCP server connection&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Verify the connection by running a test extraction in Kiro CLI:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;extract text from s3://your-bucket-name/sample.pdf&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="security-considerations"&gt;Security considerations&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Security is integrated from the beginning, not added as an afterthought. Here is how the solution handles it:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;IAM integration: The solution uses your existing AWS credentials. You do not need to create or manage separate API keys.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Least privilege access: You grant only &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; read permissions, scoped to the specific buckets that contain your PDF documents. Nothing more.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Temporary storage: The server deletes downloaded files automatically after it completes processing. No PDF data lingers on the local file system.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;No data persistence: Text extraction occurs on demand without storing results.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Audit trail: &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cloudtrail/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS CloudTrail&lt;/a&gt; logs &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; access requests for your account.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="performance-and-limitations"&gt;Performance and limitations&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Here is what to expect in terms of performance:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The server processes documents in real time. For a typical 50-page text-based PDF, results are generally available in a few seconds, making it practical for interactive workflows where you ask follow-up questions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Processing time scales linearly with document size. A 10-page document processes roughly 5 times faster than a 50-page one.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Memory usage is proportional to document size. For most text-based PDFs under 100 pages, memory consumption stays well within typical development machine limits.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This approach has clear limits. Know them before you commit:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Text-based PDFs only. If your documents are scanned images or photographs of paper, the server cannot read them. &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/textract/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt; handles those cases natively with OCR.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;No OCR capability. The server reads embedded text from the PDF file format. It cannot interpret pixels in an image.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Limited layout understanding. The server performs straightforward text extraction. It does not reconstruct tables, columns, or complex page layouts. &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/textract/latest/dg/layoutresponse.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt; handles this natively.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;No form processing. If your PDFs contain fillable form fields or structured data, the server does not extract those elements. &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/textract/latest/dg/layoutresponse.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt; handles this natively.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="real-world-use-cases"&gt;Real-world use cases&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;These capabilities translate directly into measurable outcomes across industries. Whether it’s legal teams retrieving contract clauses mid-call, compliance officers locating policy language during audits, or executives pulling earnings data in real time, the solution removes the friction of manual document search. The following examples show how different teams put it to work.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="legal-services-firm"&gt;Legal services firm&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A mid-sized legal firm adopted this solution for contract review. Their attorneys used to spend 15 to 20 minutes searching through PDF contracts to find specific indemnification clauses during client calls. That meant putting the client on hold or promising to call back later. Now they type a question into &lt;a href="https://kiro.dev/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Kiro CLI&lt;/a&gt; and get the relevant passage in seconds. The firm reports that research time during client calls was significantly reduced.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="financial-services-compliance"&gt;Financial services compliance&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A regional bank deployed the solution for regulatory examinations. During audits, compliance officers need to locate specific policy language quickly. Previously, they bookmarked key sections manually across dozens of PDF files, which was error-prone and hard to maintain as policies changed. With the MCP server connected to their S3 document repository, they now pull up the exact paragraph an examiner asks about in real time.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="corporate-strategy-team"&gt;Corporate strategy team&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;An enterprise leadership team uses the solution during quarterly strategy meetings. When a board member asks about a specific metric from the previous quarter’s earnings report, the team queries the PDF on the spot instead of flipping through printed copies. This keeps discussions moving and grounded in actual data.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="scaling-and-enhancement-options"&gt;Scaling and enhancement options&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This solution is a starting point. As your needs grow, you can extend it. Start with caching if your team accesses the same documents repeatedly. Consider batch processing when you need to handle hundreds of documents at once. Add vector search when keyword matching is no longer sufficient.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Specifically, you can extend the solution in these ways:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Add caching with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/dynamodb/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon DynamoDB&lt;/a&gt; for frequently accessed documents.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Implement batch processing with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sqs/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Simple Queue Service&lt;/a&gt; (Amazon SQS) for bulk operations.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Integrate vector search with Amazon OpenSearch Service for semantic document discovery.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create hybrid workflows that route complex documents to Amazon Textract automatically.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Add monitoring with Amazon CloudWatch to track usage patterns and error rates.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="cleanup"&gt;Cleanup&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When you’re done testing or want to remove the solution, follow these steps to avoid unnecessary costs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Stop the MCP Server&lt;/strong&gt;Press Ctrl+C in the terminal where the server is running.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Remove the MCP Configuration&lt;/strong&gt;Open your Kiro CLI MCP configuration file (&lt;code&gt;~/.kiro/settings/tools/mcp.json&lt;/code&gt;) and delete the &lt;code&gt;s3-pdf-extractor&lt;/code&gt; entry. Save and close the file.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Delete the project files&lt;/strong&gt;Remove the project directory and all its contents: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;rm -rf ~/s3-pdf-extractor&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;p&gt;Warning: This command permanently deletes all files in the directory without confirmation. Make sure you have saved any modifications before proceeding.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Clean up S3 resources (optional)&lt;/strong&gt;If you created test PDFs in Amazon S3 specifically for this walkthrough, delete the test files or the test bucket using the Amazon S3 console or the AWS CLI: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 rm s3://your-bucket-name/test-file.pdf&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;p&gt;Only delete resources you created for testing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Review IAM permissions (optional)&lt;/strong&gt;Navigate to the IAM console and remove any S3 read permissions added specifically for this solution. Keep permissions that other workflows depend on.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Verify cleanup&lt;/strong&gt;Confirm the directory no longer exists: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;ls ~/s3-pdf-extractor&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;p&gt;Expected output: No such file or directory&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After cleanup, you will no longer incur S3 storage and data transfer charges for the resources you deleted. For detailed pricing information, see Amazon S3 Pricing. If you want to redeploy later, repeat the installation steps. All code and configuration examples remain in this document.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you built an MCP server that extracts text from PDF files in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt; in real time. You walked through the architecture, compared costs with Amazon Textract, and saw how 3 different teams put this approach to work. The pattern follows a clear approach: connect your AI assistant to your documents, keep the infrastructure minimal, and scale up only when the workload demands it.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In summary, the MCP server pattern is a focused, interactive complement to Amazon Textract. Use it when an AI assistant needs to read text-based PDFs in real time. When your needs include OCR, forms, tables, or production-scale processing, Amazon Textract is the AWS service designed for that work, and the two approaches fit cleanly together. This is exactly the pattern shown in the hybrid workflow option earlier in this post.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Next steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Evaluate your use case against the criteria in the “Where this approach fits alongside Amazon Textract” section.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Deploy the solution in your development environment by following the Installation section in this post. Test with 5 to 10 representative documents to establish baseline performance.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Explore &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/textract/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt; for OCR capabilities, or learn more about &lt;a href="https://kiro.dev/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Kiro CLI&lt;/a&gt; integration as your requirements evolve.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;If you try this solution or adapt it for your own use case, we’d love to hear about it in the comments.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To learn more, explore the following resources:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/textract/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/comprehend/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Comprehend&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon S3&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://kiro.dev/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Kiro CLI&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/iam/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM)&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cloudtrail/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS CloudTrail&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://modelcontextprotocol.io/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Model Context Protocol (MCP)&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt;

		<title>How Cara pioneers domain-specific AI for enterprise insurance brokerages with AWS</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/how-cara-pioneers-domain-specific-ai-for-enterprise-insurance-brokerages-with-aws/</link>

				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>

					<description>In this post, we explore how Cara, built in cooperation with AWS, addresses these challenges. We walk through the technical design decisions and the AWS services that support the solution. We also share measurable outcomes Cara has delivered for enterprise brokerages.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Insurance is an $8 trillion global industry burdened by manual workflows and a growing talent shortage. &lt;a href="https://www.getcara.ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Cara&lt;/a&gt; delivers an AI-native solution on AWS that automates back-office processes for insurance brokerages.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Insurance agents routinely spend hours on repetitive tasks. These include completing applications, analyzing policy coverages, re-keying data across systems, and relaying information between clients and carriers. As the industry faces a persistent talent shortage, brokerages need to scale revenue without proportional headcount increases.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we explore how Cara, built in cooperation with AWS, addresses these challenges. We walk through the technical design decisions and the AWS services that support the solution. We also share measurable outcomes Cara has delivered for enterprise brokerages.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="the-challenge-why-generic-ai-falls-short-in-insurance"&gt;The challenge: Why generic AI falls short in insurance&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Insurance brokerages operate in a highly regulated environment. Every transaction demands precision, auditability, and compliance. The data involved includes sensitive personally identifiable information (PII), financial records, and underwriting details.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Generic AI tools are not designed for this complexity. Effective AI for insurance must understand domain-specific data models and brokerage workflows. It must also handle carrier-specific requirements and regulatory constraints while meeting enterprise security standards.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Cara’s founding team saw these gaps firsthand. Vic Yeh, Nikhil Kansal, and Jon Patel previously founded a digital insurance brokerage. They scaled and sold it to The McGowan Companies, one of the largest privately held insurance organizations in the US.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;During that experience, the team built an internal AI copilot powered by large language models (LLMs). The copilot reduced turnaround times, improved data accuracy, and streamlined agent workflows. Encouraged by strong adoption, they expanded the concept into a standalone product: Cara.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="architecture-overview"&gt;Architecture overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Cara is built on AWS services chosen for reliability, scalability, and security. Figure 1 shows the high-level components of Cara’s production deployment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20401-1.png" alt="Cara architecture on AWS using Amazon EKS for compute and Amazon Bedrock for inference across isolated tenant workspaces" width="600" height="496"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Cara architecture on AWS&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="compute-and-orchestration"&gt;Compute and orchestration&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Cara runs on Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service (Amazon EKS) for container orchestration across multiple Availability Zones. EKS manages Cara’s microservices, including ingestion pipelines, workflow engines, and the inference layer.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This architecture supports elastic scaling to handle demand during peak renewal and servicing periods. It supports thousands of concurrent users and workflows per brokerage. Each organization’s workloads run in isolated namespaces for tenant separation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="ai-and-inference"&gt;AI and inference&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Cara’s AI capabilities are powered by LLMs hosted on Amazon Bedrock. Amazon Bedrock provides access to foundation models through a fully managed API. This allows Cara to run inference without managing GPU infrastructure. Cara uses Amazon Bedrock for several core capabilities:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Coverage and quote intelligence&lt;/strong&gt; – compares carrier quotes, summarizes coverage differences, and highlights exclusions or gaps.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Application and form automation&lt;/strong&gt; – cross-fills ACORD and supplemental forms using source documents, prior submissions, and agency guidelines.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Proposal and renewal generation&lt;/strong&gt; – produces branded, client-ready proposals and renewal spreadsheets.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Knowledge-driven workflows&lt;/strong&gt; – references agency-specific guidelines, carrier appetites, and historical placements to guide decisions.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="security-and-data-isolation"&gt;Security and data isolation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Data protection is a foundational requirement for insurance organizations. Cara’s architecture uses account-specific deployments on AWS. Each brokerage’s data and workflows are isolated within dedicated, secure workspaces. This design supports compliance with industry regulations and provides auditability at the organization level.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="integrations"&gt;Integrations&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Cara integrates with leading agency management systems (AMS) and customer relationship management (CRM) tools. It syncs accounts, policies, and documents to reduce duplicate data entry. AI-driven workflows operate directly within existing broker technology stacks. This design helps minimize changes to the systems their agents already use.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="deployment-and-operational-characteristics"&gt;Deployment and operational characteristics&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;One of Cara’s design goals is fast time-to-value. Enterprise brokerages can get onboarded within hours and launch customized workflows within days. Cara’s deployment on EKS uses parameterized templates for each new tenant. It provisions isolated namespaces, storage, and inference endpoints without manual setup.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In production, Cara’s infrastructure on AWS provides:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;High availability&lt;/strong&gt; – multi-AZ deployment on EKS with automated failover.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Elastic scaling&lt;/strong&gt; – Kubernetes Horizontal Pod Autoscaler adjusts capacity based on real-time demand. This supports thousands of concurrent users during peak periods.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Enterprise security&lt;/strong&gt; – data isolation per tenant, encryption at rest and in transit, and integration with AWS Identity and Access Management (AWS IAM).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="measurable-outcomes"&gt;Measurable outcomes&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Cara’s AI-driven workflows have delivered quantifiable results for enterprise insurance brokerages:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table style="height: 222px" border="1px" width="911" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Metric&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Result&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Time saved per user&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;~10 hours per week through workflow automation and contextual knowledge retrieval&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Onboarding speed&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Enterprise brokerages onboarded within hours; custom workflows live within days&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt;

 &lt;/tbody&gt;

&lt;p&gt;These outcomes come from organization-specific workflow automation and contextual knowledge retrieval. They depend on Cara’s domain-specific AI and the scalable, secure infrastructure provided by AWS.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="looking-ahead"&gt;Looking ahead&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The insurance industry remains in the early stages of AI adoption. As enterprise demand grows, Cara continues to expand its AI-driven workflows across sales, servicing, and operations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;“We are thrilled to advance the boundaries of domain-specific AI in real-world insurance use cases with AWS,” says Vic Yeh, CEO of Cara. “Our goal is to help insurance professionals return to the core of our industry: the relationships.”&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we showed how Cara built a domain-specific AI solution for insurance brokerages using Amazon EKS and Amazon Bedrock. The architecture delivers tenant-isolated, elastically scaling workspaces. It supports thousands of concurrent users while meeting the security and compliance requirements of the insurance industry.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To learn more about building AI-powered applications on AWS, visit the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/architecture/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Architecture Center.&lt;/a&gt; To get started with Amazon Bedrock, see &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/getting-started/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Getting started with Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt;. For Amazon EKS, see &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/eks/getting-started/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Getting started with Amazon EKS&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
